sp=true>\n\n\nTainted fruit blamed for Bangladesh Nipah outbreaks\n---------------------------------------------------\nHONG KONG: Bangladeshis who fell sick with the new anddangerous Nipah virus\nin recent years probably caught it from eating contaminated fruit or from\ncontact with infected domestic animals, scientists said.\n\nThere have been 9 Nipah outbreaks in Bangladesh since 2001, resulting in\nthe deaths of between 40 and 100 per cent of infected people, said Jahangir\nHossain, of the Dhaka Hospital, at the International Centre for Diarrheal\nDisease Research in Bangladesh. 'Three outbreaks in Bangladesh were caused\nwhen people ate fresh date palm sap, a local sweet delicacy, which had been\ncontaminated by bats,' Hossain said in a statement issued by the Society\nfor General Microbiology. 'Because both people and animals in Bangladesh\noften eat fresh date palm sap and fruits which have been bitten by bats,\ncontaminated food and domestic animals form an important transmission\npathway for Nipah virus to infect people.'\n\nFruit bats are believed to be a natural reservoir of Nipah virus, which was\nfirst seen when it killed more than 100 people out of 257 infected in\nMalaysia in 1999. According to local media reports the virus has killed at\nleast 89 people in Bangladesh since 2001. Hossain said the first outbreaks\nin Malaysia started when pigs on farms ate fruit which had been bitten by\ninfected bats. The pigs later developed coughs and breathing difficulties,\nand the virus soon spread to pig farmers and abattoir workers.\n\nThe deadly brain and lung disease is now spreading into rural India and\nBangladesh. In one outbreak in Bangladesh, people became infected after\ncontact with sick cows, while close contact with pig herds was associated\nwith another outbreak.\n\nHossain's team is working with date palm sap collectors to stop bats from\ncontaminating date palm sap. 'If we can identify the fact to be passed from\nbats to humans so frequently, we might be able to help intervene and\ninterfere with the transmission pathways,' he said. 'Current efforts should\nfocus on restricting the consumption of fruit bitten by bats, restricting\nhuman contact with sick animals, and protecting date palm sap from\ncontamination by bat secretions.'\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Brent Barrett\n\n[This report confirms the suspected important role of contaminated date\npalm juice in the transmission of Nipah virus infection from bats to humans\nin east Bengal. Transmission of Nipah virus to humans via sick cattle has\nnot been described previously (as far as I am aware) and further details\nwould be welcomed. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "Current efforts should\nfocus on restricting the consumption of fruit bitten by bats, restricting\nhuman contact with sick animals, and protecting date palm sap from\ncontamination by bat secretions.'\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Brent Barrett\n\n A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Thu 3 Apr 2008\n Because both people and animals in Bangladesh\noften eat fresh date palm sap and fruits which have been bitten by bats,\ncontaminated food and domestic animals form an important transmission\npathway for Nipah virus to infect people.' According to the\nupazila health complex sources, [The upazilas are the lowest level of\nadministrative government in Bangladesh. - Mod.CP] The 16-year-old\nson of a Mangalpur villager started vomiting after he took date juice\non Monday [3 Mar 20088].\n\nMeanwhile, health experts of the Institute of Epidemiology Disease\nControl and Research (IEDCR) and ICDDR,B in a study report said that\nbasic preventive measures like hand washing, not sharing beds or food\nand minimising the number of contacts can limit person-to-person\ntransmission of Nipah virus that still remains a risk. The central\nand northwestern part of the country remains at risk for future\noutbreaks of Nipah virus especially in January through May, the report warned.\n\nSo far in the past weeks, 8 people, including 4 children, have died\nand 10 others badly affected in the latest outbreak of the Nipah\nvirus in Manikganj and Rajbari. The IEDCR confirmed the 1st spell of\nthe Nipah outbreak on 29 Feb 2008 based on the samples collected from\nthe bodies of the 3 children of Bishnupur village at Daulatpur\nupazila in Manikganj.\n\nNipah virus, a new member of the 'paramyxovirus' family, was named\nafter Sungai Nipah, one of the Malaysian villages affected by the\nvirus. Dr. Chua Kaw Bing from the University of Malaya discovered the\nvirus in 1999. The IEDCR and ICDDR,B study suggested that when\nclinicians see patients with fever and mental status change, they\nshould inquire if anyone else in their community has experienced a\nsimilar illness in the preceding 2 weeks. Any clustering encephalitis\ncases should be immediately reported to the district authority, it\nalso suggested. The repeated outbreaks of Nipah virus in the country\nthat result from person to person transmission underscored the\nimportance of developing strategies to interrupt the transmission of\nsaliva transmitted viruses, both at home and in hospitals, said the study.\n\nOur Faridpur correspondent said the deceased teenager was 1st\nadmitted to Rajbari sadar hospital and later shifted to Faridpur\nMedical College Hospital as his condition deteriorated. He died at\nFaridpur Medical College Hospital on Thursday evening [6 Mar 2008].\nNecessary samples from the affected boy have been sent to Dhaka for\ndetermining the exact cause of his death, said local physicians.\nMeanwhile, local authorities start a campaign today advising people\nto remain alert about Nipah virus.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This 2nd report from Bangladesh adds little to the preceding report\nciting 8 deaths in an outbreak of Nipah virus infection in the\nManikganj and Rajbari districts of west-central Bangladesh (see:\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh: (Dhaka) 20080303.0873). Faridpur civil surgeon Dr Bashirul\nIslam stated that the Institute of Epidemiology, Disease Control and\nResearch and National Influenza Centre, Bangladesh (IEDCR) yesterday [19\nJan 2010] confirmed the death of the 3 family members [as a result of Nipah\nvirus infection] following exposure to bat secretions [probably in\ncontaminated date juice].\n\nThe deceased were a 30 year old woman of Algi village under Bhanga upazila,\nher 11 year old daughter, and 10 year old nephew. Family sources said the\nwoman was the wife of a fisherman and she died on 13 Jan 2010 after she had\na fever and headache. The same day her daughter was admitted to Bhanga\nHealth Complex with the same symptoms. Later she was transferred to\nFaridpur Medical College Hospital where she died on 16 Jan 2010. Her nephew\nalso died of the same disease on 17 Jan 2010 at the Faridpur Medical\nCollege Hospital. All the 3 had consumed raw date juice before they\ncontracted the disease, family sources added.\n\nA special team from the International Centre for Diarrhoeal Disease\nResearch, Bangladesh (ICDDRB) led by Faridpur civil surgeon Dr Bashirul\nIslam visited the village on Saturday [16 Jan 2010] and collected blood\nsamples from the 3 victims. After examining the blood sample the IEDCR\nauthorities [confirmed] the disease as [Nipah virus-associated]\nencephalitis, stated Dr Bashirul, adding that the disease originated from\nraw date juice contaminated by bat [secretions].\n\nDr Bashirul advised that all residents of the district when collecting date\njuice should cover the mouth of jars with nets so that bats cannot sink\ntheir tongues into the jar. [According to the WHO fact sheet on Nipah virus\ninfection, freshly collected date palm juice should also be boiled and\nfruits should be thoroughly washed and peeled before consumption. - Mod.CP]\n\nWhile talking to The Daily Star, director of the IEDCR Prof Mahmudur Rahman\nsaid this is the 1st incidence of death due to Nipah virus this year\n[2010]. Last year [2009] encephalitis broke out in Rajbari and Manikganj\ndistricts. But very few people died because of the government's early\ndetection programme. Under this programme regular surveillance has been in\nplace in Faridpur, Rajbari, and Madaripur since 2006.\n\nA major outbreak took place in Faridpur in 2004 causing death to at least\n17 people of Laxmipur village in Sadar upazila.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Nipah virus first surfaced in Malaysia in 1998 with a mortality rate of 40\nper cent. It reappeared in Bangladesh in February 2004, spreading across 6\ndistricts of Bangladesh and claiming 17 human lives. A later outbreak in\nthe Faridpur district killed 18 of a total 30 cases -- a 60 per cent\nmortality rate .\n\nNipah virus is an emerging zoonotic virus (a virus transmitted to humans\nfrom animals). In infected people, Nipah virus causes severe illness\ncharacterized by inflammation of the brain (encephalitis) or respiratory\ndiseases. During the initial outbreaks in Malaysia and Singapore, most\nhuman infections resulted from direct contact with sick pigs or their\ncontaminated tissues. Transmission is thought to have occurred via\nrespiratory droplets, contact with throat or nasal secretions from the\npigs, or contact with the tissue of a sick animal. In the Bangladeshi and\nIndia outbreaks, consumption of fruits or fruit products (such as raw date\npalm juice) contaminated with urine or saliva from infected fruit bats was\nthe most likely source of infection. During the later outbreaks in\nBangladesh and India, Nipah virus spread directly from human to human\nthrough close contact with people's secretions and excretions. In Siliguri,\nIndia, transmission of the virus was also reported within a healthcare\nsetting, where 75 per cent of cases occurred among hospital staff or\nvisitors. From 2001 to 2008, around half of reported cases in Bangladesh\nwere due to human to human transmission. On the index farm of that\noutbreak, fruit trees were planted directly above pigs sties, and\ntransmission could have occurred via dropped fruit, as suggested in\nthe news report, or through contamination of the pig sties with urine\nor feces from fruit bats feeding in the trees above. Virus viability\nexperiments suggest that Nipah virus remains viable for up to 4 days\nin fruit bat urine at 22 deg C / 71.6 deg F (and up to one day at 37\ndeg C / 98.6 deg F) [1], so contamination of sties via urine from\ninfected bats is a plausible route of transmission. In Malaysia, the\nvirus has been isolated from swabs of fruit bat urine and partially\neaten fruit dropped by Island flying foxes (_Pteropus hypomelanus_) [2].\n\nI do not know the status of virus isolation from bats in Bangladesh;\nhowever, I am familiar with the literature on risk factors for human\ninfection in Bangladesh. The statement that: 'In one outbreak in\nBangladesh, people became infected after contact with sick cows' is\nmost likely a reference to the finding that contact with sick cows\nwas identified as a significant risk factor in a case-control study\nof an outbreak that occurred in 2001 in Chandpur village, Meherpur,\nBangladesh [3]. The role of date palm sap contamination in Nipah\nvirus transmission in Bangladesh was first suggested by Luby et al.\nin relation to an outbreak occurring in Tangail District, Bangladesh\nin 2004-2005 [4]. The article gives an interesting description and\ndiscussion of date palm sap harvesting practices for anyone who is\ninterested in further details.\n\nReferences\n----------\n1. Fogarty et al: Henipavirus susceptibility to environmental\nvariables. Virus Res. 2008 Mar; 132(1-2): 140-4 [abstract available\nat <http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18166242>]\n2. Chua et al: Isolation of Nipah virus from Malaysian Island flying\nfoxes. Microbes Infect,. 2002 Feb; 4(2): 145-151 [abstract available\nat <http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11880045>]\n3. Hsu et al: Nipah virus encephalitis reemergence, Bangladesh. Emerg\nInfect Dis. 2004 Dec; 10(12): 2082-7 [abstract available at\n<http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15663842>]\n4. Luby et al: Foodborne transmission of Nipah virus, Bangladesh.\nEmerg Infect Dis. 2006 Dec; 12(12): 1888-1894 [abstract available at\n<http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17326940>]\n\n--\nJuliet Pulliam, PhD\nCenter for Disease Ecology\n& Department of Biology\nEmory University\nAtlanta, GA 30322\nUSA\n<jrpulli@emory.edu>\n\n[ProMED-mail thanks Dr Pulliam for provision of this documentation. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "[abstract available at\n<http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17326940>]\n\n--\nJuliet Pulliam, PhD\nCenter for Disease Ecology\n& Department of Biology\nEmory University\nAtlanta, GA 30322\n I would like to help clarify what is known about Nipah virus\ntransmission from bats and to identify some sources behind various\nstatements made in the news report from Reuters India ('Tainted fruit\nblamed for Bangladesh Nipah outbreaks') included in the most recent\nposting on Nipah virus in Bangladesh (Nipah virus - Bangladesh (04):\ntransmission 20080406.1263). A child, 9, and an adult, 55, died at Rajbari Sadar\nHospital on Saturday. Another child, 11, died on Thursday [28 Feb\n2008] in Rajbari, while 3 children of a family died in Manikganj. The\nInstitute of Epidemiology Diseases Control and Research on Saturday\n[1 Mar 2008] confirmed that all the 6 deaths were caused by Nipah\nvirus, which staged a comeback after a gap of 3 years and was now\nconcentrated on 2 districts.\n\n[The institute said in a press release 3 Nipah-infected persons have\nbeen cured while 2 others are undergoing treatment at Rajbari Sadar\nHospital]. Biochemical tests carried out in IEDCR (Institute of\nEpidemiology, Disease Control & Research) laboratory detected Nipah\nvirus, which causes encephalitis and spreads to humans mainly by\nfruit bats.\n\nVirologists said the virus causes fatal inflammation of the brain,\nassociated with high fever often leading to unconsciousness. They\nadvised people in the Nipah-prevalent areas not to eat fruits bitten\nor half-bitten by fruit bats, or drink raw juices of palm and date\ntrees. They also suggested that date juice should not be collected in\nopen pitchers. Two teams from IEDCR have been working in the\nNipah-affected areas. A team comprising medical officers Sharmin\nSultana and Nusrat Humaira was sent to Rajbari. The institute's\nsenior scientific officer Saima Afroz is leading another team in\nManikganj.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2]\nDate: Mon 3 Mar 2008\nSource: Agence France-Presse (AFP) [edited]\n<http://afp.google.com/article/ALeqM5gt5QjVGTQocukuhSmfPd7aAksavg>\n\n\nThe deadly Nipah virus has returned to Bangladesh, killing at least 8\npeople since the latest outbreak was confirmed, officials said Sunday\n[1 Mar 2008]. Nipah induces flu-like symptoms that often lead to\nencephalitis and coma, with at least a 70 percent mortality rate in\nBangladesh. The latest outbreak was confirmed on Friday [29 Feb 2008].\n\n'So far 8 people including 4 children have died in the latest\noutbreak of the Nipah virus in (central) Manikganj and Rajbari\ndistricts,' said Mahmudur Rahman, a senior health official. 'Several\nothers are in critical condition. We told people not to be panicked.\nWe have also increased surveillance in the affected area,' he said.\n\nAt least 89 people have died of the virus since the 1st outbreak in\n2001. The Bangladesh government has set up a detection laboratory to\nspeed up testing for any outbreak. In 2004, the same 2 districts\nreported the worst outbreak, when nearly 40 people died, and the\nUnited States Center for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) was\ncalled in to help combat the disease.\n\nThe virus, named after the Malaysian village where it was first\ndetected, jumped the species barrier from fruit bats to pigs and then\nto humans in October 1998. It is believed to be caught through direct\ncontact with pigs. In 1999, 256 people in Malaysia fell ill with the\ndisease, and 4 in 10 patients died. More than a million pigs were\nslaughtered to help curb its spread.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Nipah virus is a member of the family _Paramyxoviridae_. Its name\noriginated from Sungai Nipah, a village in the Malaysian Peninsula\nwhere pig farmers became ill with encephalitis. Nipah virus was first\nreported in pigs and thence humans in Malaysia in 1998. The disease\nin pigs was relatively mild. Human cases of Nipah virus infection,\napparently unassociated with infection in livestock, have been\nreported in Bangladesh since 2001. Species of fruit bats (genus\n_Pteropus_) have been identified as natural hosts of Nipah virus.\nAnthropogenic changes (habitat loss, hunting) that have impacted the\npopulation dynamics of _Pteropus_ species across much of their range\nare hypothesised to have facilitated emergence.\n\nNipah virus has been transmitted to humans, cats, and dogs through\nclose contact with infected pigs. However, person-to-person\ntransmission can occur. An encephalitis outbreak was investigated in\nFaridpur District, Bangladesh, in April-May 2004. 36 cases of Nipah\nvirus illness were identified; 75 percent of case-patients died.\nMultiple peaks of illness occurred, and 33 case-patients had close\ncontact with another Nipah virus patient. RT-PCR (reverse\ntranscriptase polymerase chain reaction) testing of environmental\nsamples confirmed Nipah virus contamination of hospital surfaces.\nThis investigation provided evidence for person-to-person\ntransmission of Nipah virus [see: Gurley ES, Montgomery JM, Hossain\nMJ, Bell M, Azad AK, Islam MR, et al: Person-to-person transmission\nof Nipah virus in a Bangladeshi community. Emerg Infect Dis. 2007\nJul; 13(7): 1031-7, available at\n<http://www.cdc.gov/EID/content/13/7/1031.htm>].\n\nNipah virus illness begins with 3-14 days of fever and headache\nfollowed by drowsiness and disorientation characterized by mental\nconfusion. These signs and symptoms can progress to coma within 24-48\nhours. Some patients have had a respiratory illness during the early\npart of their infections.\n\nDuring the Nipah virus disease outbreak in 1998-99 in Malaysia, 257\npatients were infected with the virus. About 40 percent of those\npatients who entered hospitals with serious nervous disease died from\nthe illness.\n\nAn interactive HealthMap/ProMED-mail map of Bangladesh can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/promed?v=23.8,90.3,5>.\nA map of the districts of Bangladesh showing Manikganj and Rajbari to\nthe west of the capital Dhaka is available at\n<http://www.thp.org/bangladesh/map.htm>. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\n[1]\n This investigation provided evidence for person-to-person\ntransmission of Nipah virus [see: Gurley ES, Montgomery JM, Hossain\nMJ, Bell M, Azad AK, Islam MR, et al: Person-to-person transmission\nof Nipah virus in a Bangladeshi community. Their blood samples have been sent to the\nNational Institute of Virology, Pune, for confirmatory tests.\nOf the 30 patients, 20 are from Betai, close to the Bangladesh border,\nabout 150 km from Calcutta. Nadia chief medical officer Mrinal Kanti Biswas\nsaid the decision to procure ribavarin was taken following advice from\nexperts at the Pune institute. 'We are buying the drug from Hyderabad and\nwill distribute it free to those who show symptoms of the affliction or run\ntemperature for days,' said Biswas.\nA 3 member medical board has been set up to monitor fever cases being\nreported to hospitals and health centres in the district. Suspected\npatients will be kept under observation in isolation wards. Besides a\ndistrict hospital in Krishnagar, Nadia has 4 subdivisional hospitals and 7\nblock hospitals.\nNipah has no cure, but ribavarin would alleviate the symptoms of nausea,\nvomiting, and convulsion, said doctors. The virus, which has claimed 3\nlives in the district and afflicted many, could have come from Bangladesh.\nTapas Majumder, the first victim from Betai, had been to the neighbouring\ncountry on a business trip in February. 'He was running a temperature\nwithin days of his return,' district magistrate S Meena said.\nThe virus, 1st detected in Malaysia in 1999, has since been known to strike\nonly 2 other places, Bangladesh and Bengal. It killed 105 of the 265\npatients in Malaysia, 18 of 30 in Bangladesh in April 2004, and [75 per\ncent] of the 66 it infected in Siliguri in 2001.\n(byline: Rabi Banerjee)\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n[Ribavirin (Virazole) is a nucleoside analog considered to have broad\nspectrum antiviral activity against several DNA- and RNA-containing\nviruses. It is relatively toxic and accordingly its use has been\nrestricted. Notably it has been used in life-threatening respiratory\nsyncytial virus infection in infancy, against certain exotic viruses such\nas Lassa fever virus and more recently Nipah virus, and in combination with\npegylated interferon against certain genotypes of hepatitis C virus.\nAlthough first synthesized in 1972 its node of antiviral activity is\nunclear. Among other effects, ribavirin monophosphate is a competitive\ninhibitor of cellular monophosphate dehydrogenase which results in\nreduction of GTP (guanosine triphosphate) pools in treated cells. The use\nof ribavirin for treatment of the symptoms of suspected Nipah virus\ninfection in patients in West Bengal with undefined fevers seems a rather\nextreme responds at this juncture. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mailis a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n Notably it has been used in life-threatening respiratory\nsyncytial virus infection in infancy, against certain exotic viruses such\nas Lassa fever virus and more recently Nipah virus, and in combination with\npegylated interferon against certain genotypes of hepatitis C virus.\n (byline: Rabi Banerjee)\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n The use\nof ribavirin for treatment of the symptoms of suspected Nipah virus\ninfection in patients in West Bengal with undefined fevers seems a rather\nextreme responds at this juncture. Researchers from the Dhaka-based Institute of\nEpidemiology, Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) suspected Nipah\nvirus because last year's outbreak was caused by contact with fruit\nbats, which carry the virus and can transmit it to people. The team\nthinks the juice was made from fruit contaminated by fruit bat\ndroppings, or from fruit the bats had half-eaten.\nThe researchers sent samples of patients' blood to the Centers for\nDisease Control and Prevention in Atlanta, United States, which\nconfirmed Nipah virus infection in one of them. Bangladesh does not\nhave adequate research facilities for diagnosing the virus or working\non ways of controlling it. One constraint is that there are no\nlaboratories in which the virus can be studied safely, without a risk\nof it escaping and infecting more people.\nThe IEDCR, however, is trying to find out how the outbreak began.\nAlthough the researchers suspect fruit bats are the main carriers of\nNipah virus, they are also collecting samples of blood, tissue and\ndroppings from other species including cats, dogs, poultry, cattle\nand rodents to see whether the virus has other hosts. By sampling\nfruit trees, they hope to determine whether the virus can be\ntransmitted through contact with fruit touched by the bats. Mahmudur\nRahman, lead researcher and director of the IEDCR, told SciDev.Net\nthat climate might also play a role in patterns of outbreaks, as each\nof the previous outbreaks also occurred in winter.\nStrong evidence of human-to-human transmission of Nipah virus was\nfound for the 1st time during 2004's outbreak, according to research\npublished in the Health and Science Bulletin of the Dhaka-based\nCentre for Health and Population Research. Because of this threat,\nthe Directorate of Health Services has advised healthcare officials\nto wear masks and gloves when treating patients suspected of having\nNipah, and the public has been warned to avoid close contact with\ninfected people.\nSymptoms of Nipah virus infection are similar to those of influenza,\nsuch as fever and muscle pain. In some cases, inflammation of the\nbrain occurs, leading to disorientation or coma. Around half of the\nsymptomatic cases are fatal. Rahman thinks there could be a\nconnection between a recent increase in Bangladesh of cases of\nencephalitis, or inflammation of the brain, and Nipah or a Nipah-like\nvirus. 'We need extensive research on encephalitis,' he says.\nThere is no cure for Nipah, but a vaccine is in development.\nAccording to Bangladesh's Directorate of Health Services, Nipah virus\nhas infected a total of 211 people and killed 59 of them since 2001,\nwhen the virus was 1st detected in the Bangladesh. The only\npreviously reported outbreaks of Nipah occurred in Malaysia and\nSingapore between September 1998 and May 1999.\n[The full paper on human-to-human transmission of Nipah virus can be\naccessed in Health and Science Bulletin 2, 5 (2004)\n<http://202.136.7.26/pub/publication.jsp?classificationID=56&pubID=5252>]\n[Byline: Mustak Hossain]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The continuing investigation, together with the observation that\nNipah virus was detected in only one of the samples sent to the CDC\nin Atlanta, suggests that doubt remains about the reservoir of Nipah\nvirus infection in Bangladesh and the circumstances surrounding its\ntransmission to humans. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n [The continuing investigation, together with the observation that\nNipah virus was detected in only one of the samples sent to the CDC\nin Atlanta, suggests that doubt remains about the reservoir of Nipah\nvirus infection in Bangladesh and the circumstances surrounding its\ntransmission to humans. Now that Nipah has\nbeen confirmed, the thread has been changed. - Mod.DK]\nBangladesh: The Nipah virus isolated in Tangail is distinct from the\nMalaysian strain\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe Nipah virus behind a mysterious sickness which has claimed several\nlives in Bangladesh is not the Malaysian strain, but is closely related to\nit, National Public Health Laboratory consultant virologist Prof. Chua Kaw\nBing said. Professor Chua, who was part of a Malaysian team invited by WHO\nto help set up a diagnostic virology laboratory in Dhaka last year [2004],\nsaid the victims probably had contracted the virus by consuming\npartially-eaten fruits contaminated by flying foxes. He was responding to a\nreport yesterday [Wed 26 Jan 2005] that disease experts in the United\nStates had [detected] Nipah virus in blood samples of afflicted persons in\nTangail, 190 km north-east of Dhaka, in Bangladesh.\nProf. Chua said that in May last year [2004], he and 2 others from\nUniversity Hospital -- Prof. Tan Chong Tin, a neurologist, and Dr. K.T.\nWong, a pathologist -- were invited by WHO to Dhaka to assist in setting up\nthe diagnostic virology laboratory, on which work is still in progress. He\nsaid that they learned that Bangladeshis were found to have contracted\nNipah virus infection in various places, in Mahepur in 2001, Nagoan in\n2003, and Faripur (twice in 2004).\nIn Bangladesh, the victims are known to have contracted Nipah virus in the\nearly part of the year. This may have something to do with the ecology of\nthe region, such as certain fruits ripening at that time and the birthing\nseason of the flying foxes. 'It is a complicated process' he said.\nIn the Faripur outbreak, Prof. Chua said Bangladeshi epidemiologists\nsuspected that the victims could have contracted Nipah virus through\ncontaminated fruits that were partially eaten by flying foxes. 'This\nsuspicion is based on my work of isolating the Nipah virus (in 1999/2000)\nfrom partially eaten fruits by flying foxes of _pteropid_ species.' The\nhealth authorities in Dhaka, he added, should [carry out further\ninvestigations] to have a better understanding of how the virus has spilled\nover from flying foxes to humans, as the situation there could be quite\ndifferent from the situation in Malaysia.\n[Byline: Majorie Chiew]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>", "summary": "The\nhealth authorities in Dhaka, he added, should [carry out further\ninvestigations] to have a better understanding of how the virus has spilled\nover from flying foxes to humans, as the situation there could be quite\ndifferent from the situation in Malaysia.\n A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n Professor Chua, who was part of a Malaysian team invited by WHO\nto help set up a diagnostic virology laboratory in Dhaka last year [2004],\nsaid the victims probably had contracted the virus by consuming\npartially-eaten fruits contaminated by flying foxes. The Nadia infection, which now\nafflicts at least 30 people, was 1st thought to be a new strain of dengue.\nBut blood samples of the 3 dead patients have tested positive for Nipah\n[virus] at the National Institute of Virology in Pune [in the western\nMaharashtra state of India].\nThe wife of one of the 3 dead confirmed that bats are common in her\nKrishnagar locality. 'They even enter our home and bite us, but we never\nbothered about it before,' she said. [Nipah virus is transmitted to humans\nand pigs via bat urine and saliva-contaminated fruit, not usually by bites.\n- Mod.CP] She has a temperature and is undergoing regular check-ups at the\ndistrict hospital. 'Fruit bats are its [Nipah virus's] natural hosts,' a\nvirologist said. 'The bats can infect pigs and both can infect humans.\nTransmission requires close contact with infected tissues or body fluids.\nWe have asked families to cut the leaves of fruit plants and stop eating\npork,' said Mohan Basu, deputy chief medical officer of heath, Nadia\ndistrict. [However, it is not certain that all 30 patients have the Nipah\nvirus.]\nThe hitherto unexplained fever had afflicted 50 people since February\n[2007] but some 15 have recovered and 2 of the 5 dead actually died of\nencephalitis. No medicine has been found to work against Nipah, which\nspreads rapidly and has a high mortality rate. It killed 105 of 265\npatients in Malaysia, 18 of 30 in Bangladesh in April 2004, and 3/4 of the\n66 it infected in Siliguri [West Bengal] in 2001. In Siliguri, most\npatients were either hospital employees or visitors, suggesting\nhuman-to-human spread is a possibility. Virologists said domestic cats and\ndogs are suspected carriers, too. Death can occur in 48-72 hours. The\nsymptoms are high temperature, muscle pain, nausea, and convulsions leading\nin some cases to a coma.\n[byline: Sanjay Mandal, Rabi Banerjee]\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n******\n[2]\nDate: Tue 8 May 2007\nSource: International Herald Tribune, Associated Press report [edited]\n<http://www.iht.com/articles/ap/2007/05/08/asia/AS-MED-India-Nipah-Virus.php>\n\nThe rare, animalborne Nipah virus has killed 5 people in the eastern Indian\nstate [of West Bengal], prompting authorities to declare a state of alert,\nofficials said on Tue 8 May 2007. A health official and 4 members of a\nfamily have died from the illness since early April [2007], said Mohan\nBasu, a doctor in West Bengal state's Nadia district.\nNipah virus is usually spread by fruit bats or pigs. There have been no\nknown cases of human-to-human infection, according to the World Health\nOrganization (WHO). The last [1st - Mod.CP] major Nipah outbreak occurred\nin Malaysia, where 265 people were infected in 1998-99. The virus was then\nblamed for 105 human deaths. Nearly a million pigs, believed to have spread\nthe disease, were slaughtered before the Malaysian outbreak was controlled.\nThe virus, which causes encephalitis, is lethal in about 50 per cent of\ninfected humans. No drugs have proven effective against it. Nadia is 120 km\n(75 miles) north of West Bengal's capital, Calcutta, and borders\nBangladesh, which had a Nipah outbreak in 2004. 'Initially we thought that\nthe cases were of dengue (a mosquitoborne viral disease), but a test later\nconfirmed that it was Nipah virus that caused the deaths,' Basu said.\nThe outbreak was confirmed by the region's senior health official, Nipati\nRoy. The virus was found in samples that were sent to the National\nInstitute of Virology. 'We are sending streams of blood samples taken from\nbats and pigs and even household pets,' Basu said. 'We are keeping a close\nsurveillance.' Basu said he suspected that the virus had been spread by\nfruit bats in Nadia, and that residents have been warned to avoid the\nflying mammals.\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n[Nipah virus infection has been confirmed as the cause of 5 previously\nundiagnosed cases of illness in the Nadia district of West Bengal [see the\n4 most recent 'see alsos' below]. The precise status of the 30-50 people\nstill under surveillance is uncertain. The source of the outbreak and the\ninvolvement of bats, pigs, and domestic cats and dogs have still to be\nestablished. Bangladesh (Faridpur) 2004: trans.", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2207253", "date": "2005-01-26 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (FARIDPUR) 2004: TRANSMISSION\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Wed 26 Jan 2005\nFrom: Joseph Dudley <jdudley@intellibridge.com>\n\nA comment on person-to-person transmission of Nipah virus\n-----------------------------------------------\nExtensive human-to-human transmission of Nipah virus has been reported by\nthe International Centre for Diarrhoeal Disease Research, Bangladesh\n(ICCDRB) in conjunction with the Nipah outbreak that occurred in Faridpur,\nBangladesh during February-April 2004 [see ProMED-Mail post 'Nipah virus -\nBangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102'].\nThe ICCDRB online report states that 'Preliminary epidemiologic evidence\nclearly indicates that Nipah virus was primarily spread from\nperson-to-person during this outbreak,' and that the Faridpur disease\ncluster was associated with an acute respiratory distress syndrome not\npreviously documented as a feature of Nipah virus infection elsewhere.\nInvestigations by ICCDRB indicate that there may have been 33 cases of\ndirect human-to-human transmission of Nipah virus during the 2004 outbreak\nin Faridpur, which resulted in 36 confirmed human cases of Nipah virus\ninfection and 27 known fatalities. Most cases of human-to-human\ntransmission reportedly occurred among relatives who had cared for\nseverely-ill family members, although 2 cases of evident human transmission\nresulting from one-time exposures to infected patients are also reported\n(Reference: ICCDRB. Person-to-person transmission of Nipah virus during an\noutbreak in Faridpur District, 2004. Dudley, Ph.D.\nSenior Analyst, Biosecurity & Agriculture\nINTELLIBRIDGE\n1101 30th Street, NW Suite 100B\n Investigations by ICCDRB indicate that there may have been 33 cases of\ndirect human-to-human transmission of Nipah virus during the 2004 outbreak\nin Faridpur, which resulted in 36 confirmed human cases of Nipah virus\ninfection and 27 known fatalities.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["nipah virus"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2005-01-26 00:00:00", "2004-06-25 00:00:00", "2004-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Washington", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur District", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2205985", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus (2004) (03) - Bangladesh: background", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2205985", "date": "2004-06-25 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS (2004) (03) - BANGLADESH: BACKGROUND\n************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Thu 24 Jun 2004\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: The New Nation, Thu 24 Jun 2004 [edited]\n<http://nation.ittefaq.com/artman/publish/article_10235.shtml>\n\nThe 2004 Nipah Virus Outbreak in Bangladesh\n-------------------------------------------\nAlthough Nipah virus first surfaced in Malaysia in 1998 with a mortality\nrate of 40 percent, it reappeared in Bangladesh with greater lethality. WHO\nreports that in February 2004, an outbreak of Nipah virus spread across 6\ndistricts of Bangladesh and claimed 17 human lives of 23 cases -- a\nmortality rate of 74 percent. A 2nd outbreak in the Faridpur district\nkilled 18 of a total 30 cases -- a 60 percent mortality rate -- as of 19\nApr 2004.\nNipah virus, inducing influenza-like symptoms often leading to encephalitis\nand coma, came into view when it jumped from infected pigs in Malaysia to\nhumans, killing both. The virus was named after the Malaysian village where\nit was first discovered. Of 265 confirmed cases in Malaysia and Singapore,\n105 died. And even among the survivors, the virus has taken a horrendous\ntoll: many of the survivors were left with some degree of brain damage. To\ncontain the spread of the disease the Malaysian authorities killed over a\nmillion pigs. Currently it is believed that fruit bats (also known as\n'flying foxes') serve as the virus reservoir, based on the finding of\nnatural Nipah viral antibody in healthy fruit bats. Virus has been isolated\nfrom the urine of fruit bats. Normally, flying foxes live deep in the\njungle, feeding off succulent fruit like jambu air [also known as Jambu\nmawar, water apple, or rose apple, a small red succulent fruit with pink or\nwhite flesh] and mango. Local hunters shoot and eat them. In Cambodia, they\nare prized as aphrodisiacs, and the bats are used as good luck talismans in\nFilipino wedding ceremonies.\nSo, how did the pig acquire the virus? Researchers have tried to link the\nemergence of Nipah virus to El Nino. During 1997-1998, El Nino brought\ndrought and dry weather to the Indonesian peninsula, which led to massive\nforest fires and the haze that shrouded the region for months.\nConsequently, haze blocked sunlight, reducing the ability of trees to\nflower and bear fruit. This forced fruit bats to travel great distances in\nsearch of sustenance. They settled in places outside their usual habitat\nsuch as the fringes of forests, close to pig farms. They may have passed\nthe virus on to pigs via their urine, as well as their saliva in the\nhalf-eaten fruit that fell to the ground and was then eaten by the\nvoracious pigs. The presence of Nipah virus antibodies indicate that bats\nwere infected with the virus, as were pigs, cats, and dogs. In the\nMalaysian outbreak, pigs were the apparent source of infection for most\nhuman cases: 93 percent of the confirmed cases had occupational exposure to\npigs. In contrast, pigs farming is uncommon in the outbreak areas in\nBangladesh. That implies that other intermediary sources other than pigs\nwere the source of infection.\nIn the recent Bangladesh outbreak, fruit bats are also the suspected\nreservoir for Nipah virus. A significant number of victims in Bangladesh\nwere under age 19. It has been hypothesized that young boys may have\ncollected and eaten fruits harvested from trees [in the early morning] and\ncould have contracted infection by eating the same fruits that fruit bats\nfed on during the night. in addition many of the cases in Bangladesh have\noccurred within households -- with victims having had no direct contact\nwith animals or bats. This implicates that human-to-human transmission\nmight be possible, which is a matter of great concern. However,\nhuman-to-human transmission of Nipah virus had not been reported in the\nMalasyian outbreak.\n[Byline: Mohammad Sarowar Hossain]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[While this article provides a useful summary of the 2004 outbreak of Nipah\nvirus infection in Bangladesh, it may be premature to conclude that\nperson-to-person transmission occurred -- in this outbreak in the absence\nof reliable information concerning both the reservoir host and the actual\nroute of transmission. Transmission of infection within households may be\nindicative of a common source of infection, which could be fruit or bat\nurine-contaminated material rather than a single infected human family\nmember. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n [While this article provides a useful summary of the 2004 outbreak of Nipah\nvirus infection in Bangladesh, it may be premature to conclude that\nperson-to-person transmission occurred -- in this outbreak in the absence\nof reliable information concerning both the reservoir host and the actual\nroute of transmission. WHO\nreports that in February 2004, an outbreak of Nipah virus spread across 6\ndistricts of Bangladesh and claimed 17 human lives of 23 cases -- a\nmortality rate of 74 percent. In the\nMalaysian outbreak, pigs were the apparent source of infection for most\nhuman cases: 93 percent of the confirmed cases had occupational exposure to\npigs. They may have passed\nthe virus on to pigs via their urine, as well as their saliva in the\nhalf-eaten fruit that fell to the ground and was then eaten by the\nvoracious pigs. Normally, flying foxes live deep in the\njungle, feeding off succulent fruit like jambu air [also known as Jambu\nmawar, water apple, or rose apple, a small red succulent fruit with pink or\nwhite flesh] and mango. Transmission of infection within households may be\nindicative of a common source of infection, which could be fruit or bat\nurine-contaminated material rather than a single infected human family\nmember. It has been hypothesized that young boys may have\ncollected and eaten fruits harvested from trees [in the early morning] and\ncould have contracted infection by eating the same fruits that fruit bats\nfed on during the night. However,\nhuman-to-human transmission of Nipah virus had not been reported in the\nMalasyian outbreak.\n In Cambodia, they\nare prized as aphrodisiacs, and the bats are used as good luck talismans in\nFilipino wedding ceremonies.\n [Byline: Mohammad Sarowar Hossain]\n--\nProMED-mail\n< Currently it is believed that fruit bats (also known as\n'flying foxes') serve as the virus reservoir, based on the finding of\nnatural Nipah viral antibody in healthy fruit bats. The outbreak, which occurred in the Faridpur District of Bangladesh,\nwas responsible for 34 cases, including 26 deaths. No new cases have been\nreported since mid-April 2004. The Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC) confirmed Nipah virus infection in 16 of the cases. Health\nauthorities in Bangladesh, the International Center for Diarrheal Disease\nResearch in Bangladesh, CDC, and other international partners worked\ntogether to assess and control this outbreak. Outbreaks of Nipah virus\nencephalitis have previously occurred in Bangladesh in May\n2001, January/February 2003, and January/February 2004.\nNipah virus, which was discovered in 1999, is a zoonotic virus (infectious\ndisease that can be transmitted from certain animals to humans). Fruit bats\nof the genus _Pteropus_ are thought to be the reservoir for this virus. The\ninitial outbreak in Malaysia and Singapore affected humans and commercial\nswine herds, although other species, including cats, dogs, and horses, were\nalso infected. The virus mainly caused respiratory illness among the pigs\nand an encephalitis syndrome among humans. Nipah virus infection can cause\nfever, muscle pains (myalgia), drowsiness, and encephalitis characterized\nby serious central nervous system illness, coma, seizures, and inability to\nmaintain breathing.\nAs a general precaution, CDC advises travelers to avoid contact with wild\nor domestic animals in the region. As with other infectious illnesses, one\nof the most important and appropriate preventive practices is careful\nand frequent hand washing, which helps remove potentially infectious\nmaterials from the skin and prevents disease transmission. For more\ninformation about Nipah virus and Nipah virus encephalitis see\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/dvrd/spb/mnpages/dispages/nipah.htm> and\n<http://www.icddrb.org/pub/publication.jsp?classificationID=56&pubID=4673>\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>", "summary": "For more\ninformation about Nipah virus and Nipah virus encephalitis see\n<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/dvrd/spb/mnpages/dispages/nipah.htm> and\n<http://www.icddrb.org/pub/publication.jsp?classificationID=56&pubID=4673>\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org> A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n Nipah virus infection can cause\nfever, muscle pains (myalgia), drowsiness, and encephalitis characterized\nby serious central nervous system illness, coma, seizures, and inability to\nmaintain breathing. Now that Nipah has\nbeen confimed the thread has been changed. - Mod.DK]\nBangladesh (Faridpur): Nipah Virus Confirmed\n--------------------------------------------\nAs of Tue 20 Apr 2004, WHO has received preliminary reports of one cluster\nof 30 cases, including 18 deaths, attributed to Nipah virus infection in\nFaridpur district. Laboratory testing performed by Centers for Disease\nControl and Prevention (CDC), Atlanta has confirmed Nipah virus infection\nin 16 of the cases.\nA team comprising experts from the Institute of Epidemiology Disease\nControl and Research, Bangladesh, the International Centre for Health and\nPopulation Research (ICDDRB), Bangladesh and the WHO country office is\ncarrying out epidemiological investigations.\nThe team has developed guidelines for case management. These will be used\nat workshops planned for this week on infection control and safe clinical\nmanagement for local medical staff in different health care settings in\nFaridpur district.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n Source: World Health Organisation, CSR, Disease Outbreak News, Tue 20 Apr\n2004 [edited]\n<http://www.who.int/csr/don/2004_04_20/en/>\n\n[The previous two posts on this outbreak were 'Unexplained deaths -\nBangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941' and 'Unexplained deaths -\nBangladesh (Faridpur): Nipah suspected 20040419.1087'. As of Tue 20 Apr 2004, WHO has received preliminary reports of one cluster\nof 30 cases, including 18 deaths, attributed to Nipah virus infection in\nFaridpur district.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["nipah virus"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2004-04-20 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Faridpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Atlanta", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2206906", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus - Bangladesh (2004)(04)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2206906", "date": "2004-11-22 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2004) (04)\n***********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Sun 21 Nov 2004\nFrom: Pablo Nart <pablo11@hotpop.com>\nSource: The Independent, Bangladesh, Sun 21 Nov 2004 [edited]\n<http://independent-bangladesh.com/news/nov/21/21112004ts.htm#A1>\n\nBangladesh: possibility of person-to-person transmission considered\n-----------------------------------------------\n'Nipah,' a seasonal viral disease causing encephalitis, which claimed 50\nlives last winter [northern winter months of 2004], may strike again in a\nvirulent form, warn local and foreign experts. Last year [2003] the spread\nof the disease seemed to have stopped.\nA study conducted by local and foreign virologists recently revealed\nthat the outbreak of the virus has been limited to southwestern\ndistricts of Bangladesh in the past 3 years, with a mortality rate of\n50 to 100 percent in different areas. The virus was traced to fruit\nbats. Some experts are of the opinion that the virus-prone belt\nstretches along the course of the Padma [river] up to south-west India,\ncausing death to an unaccounted number of people every year. There is\nno laboratory in Bangladesh that can detect the virus.\nExtensive research has found that pregnant bats harbored the virus\nand possibly transmitted it to humans. The experts did not rule out\nhuman to human transmission of the disease. In the latest spell this\nyear [2004], 4 members of a mendicant family at Faridpur died, after\nthe mendicant himself became infected and died. A rickshaw-puller,\nwho carried a patient to the hospital, also became infected and died.\nThis gives credence to the view that the virus is passed on from\nhumans to humans.\nThe bats become pregnant from November to April, and most of the Nipah-like\ninfection cases were reported during that period. The study found that,\nusually, a group of people were infected at a time, and that the victims\nwere related. Dr. AKM Shahidul Islam, an Associate Professor of Microbiology\nat the Sir Salimullah Medical College Hospital, who was part of the team\nthat conducted the survey, said date and palm juices, contaminated by urine\nor droppings of Nipah-infected bats, could transmit Nipah virus to humans.\nHe said: 'The jujube drops from a tree after it had been partly eaten up by\nbats at night. While eating a jujube, the bats secrete saliva on it. When\nthe children collect the fruit, and eat it without washing, the virus of\nbat's saliva is transmitted to them.' He pointed out that most of the people\nwho died of this virus infection were not more than 15 years old. He advised\npeople not to eat fruit bitten or half-eaten by bats, or drink the raw\njuices of palm and date in a Nipah-prevalent area. About 17 percent of the\nbats were found to be carrying antibodies against Nipah virus in their blood\nand, surprisingly, all were pregnant. Tests on other domestic and wild\nanimals proved negative.\nDr. Shahid said, 'The latest finding is the presence of [Nipah] virus\ngenetic material in the beds of 2 patients in Faridpur General\nHospital. It means that the virus is capable of spilling out of the\npatient's body, but it is questionable whether it is infectious or\nnot. Only laboratory culture of the sample could prove whether these\nare full [infectious] virus or only [non-infectious material], but\nthe IEDCR has no facility to ascertain that,' he added. Dr. Shahid\nsaid: 'Several guidelines were formulated to control the outbreak of\nthe disease, including guidelines for doctors regarding diagnosis and\ntreatment, for nurses and other health-care professionals about the\ncare of patients as well as personal protective measures, and for the\npublic and relatives of the patients about what to do in case of\ninfection.'\nLast season [northern winter 2004], the disease was 1st reported in\nGoalundo, in Rajbari district, as an 'unknown'' ailment between 12 and 18\nJan 2004. Then, it was reported in Faridpur, Manikganj, Tangail, and\nJoypurhat. The disease begins with fever, followed by unconsciousness within\none to 2 days of feverish illness. According to an ICDDR [International\nCentre for Diarrhoeal Disease Research]study, 2 previous Nipah outbreaks\nwere recorded before the last outbreak. The 1st recorded outbreak occurred\nbetween 25 Apr and 26 May 2001, causing 9 deaths in Meherpur district.\nBetween 11 and 28 Jan 2003, another outbreak killed 8 out of 17 affected\npersons in Naogaon. After the epidemic in January 2004, foreign experts from\nthe Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, Atlanta, USA, the World\nHealth Organization headquarters in Geneva, Switzerland, Health Canada, and\nthe University of Malaya came to the country to investigate the outbreak.\n[Byline: Nurul Islam Hasib]\n--\nPablo Nart\n<pablo11@hotpop.com>", "summary": "Dr. Shahid\nsaid: 'Several guidelines were formulated to control the outbreak of\nthe disease, including guidelines for doctors regarding diagnosis and\ntreatment, for nurses and other health-care professionals about the\ncare of patients as well as personal protective measures, and for the\npublic and relatives of the patients about what to do in case of\ninfection.' Dr. Shahid said, 'The latest finding is the presence of [Nipah] virus\ngenetic material in the beds of 2 patients in Faridpur General\nHospital. He advised\npeople not to eat fruit bitten or half-eaten by bats, or drink the raw\njuices of palm and date in a Nipah-prevalent area.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["unknown", "other", "nipah virus"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2004-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2001-05-26 00:00:00", "2004-01-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Rajbari district", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "India", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Padma", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Independent", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Switzerland", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Geneva", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Naogaon", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Meherpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Atlanta", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2198429", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus, reservoir (02)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2198429", "date": "2000-08-15 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS, RESERVOIR (02)\n***************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org> NIPAH VIRUS, RESERVOIR (02)\n* **\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["nipah virus"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "462", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Nipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=462", "date": "2004-02-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH/HENDRA-LIKE VIRUS - BANGLADESH (RAJBARI)\n**********************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Fri 6 Feb 2004\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: The Nation, City News, Thu 5 Feb 2004 [edited]\n<http://nation.ittefaq.com/artman/publish/article_7191.shtml>\n\n[Please refer back to the previous thread on Undiagnosed deaths -\nBangladesh (Rajbari) listed in the see alsos below The illness has now\nbeen diagnosed as due to a Nipah/Hendra-like virus.- Mod .DK]\nWHO/CDC Experts to Probe Suspected Nipah/Henra-like Virus Outbreak\n------------------------------------------------------------------\nDHAKA: Four experts from WHO Geneva and Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC) of USA arrived here on Wed 4 Feb 2004 on a mission to\nprobe the source of the mysterious disease that so far has claimed 20\npeople in Bangladesh. They are Dr Eric Bertherat and Dr Alice Croisier from\nWHO in Geneva, and Dr Joel Montgomery and Dr Kathy Hanlon from CDC-Atlanta.\nAnother expert from CDC, Dr Darrin Carroll, is expected to arrive today\n(Thu 5 Feb 2004). Dr Robert F Breiman, the Head of Programme on Infectious\nDiseases and Vaccine Science of ICDDR,B, Dr Kazi AHM Akram of WHO Dhaka,\nand six more experts, including animal virologist and entomologist from DG\nHealth Services, will also work with the newly arrived team. The experts\nhad a meeting with DG Health Services Dr Mizanur Rahman in the afternoon to\nchalk out a plan for investigating the disease and for a briefing about\nBangladesh.\nExperts in Dhaka have identified the disease as viral encephalitis that has\nno link to avian influenza. Quoting a viral special pathogen laboratory\nreport conducted by Centers for Disease Control and Prevention\n(CDC)-Atlanta in USA, Health Secretary AFM Sarwar Kamal on Sun 1 Feb 2004\nsaid the outbreak of the disease was caused by a virus closely related to\nNipah and Hendra viruses [a novel group of paramyxoviruses, the former\npreviously isolated in Malaysia and Bangladesh, and the latter in Australia\n- Mod.CP]. Nipah or Hendra viruses could be linked to pigs and bats, as\ncases in Malaysia and Singapore pinpointed pigs, while bats appeared to be\na 'reservoir' for the viruses, according to Dr Robert F Breiman of ICDDR,B.\nThe experts will evaluate the source of the Nipah/Hendra-like viruses and\ndefine the mechanisms of transmission of the virus to humans - to establish\nhow the virus moved from animals to humans.\nThese viruses were the cause of two previous outbreaks of fatal\nencephalitis in Bangladesh in 2001 and 2003, according to official sources\nhere [see ProMED-mail post below]. The government found no link of the\nmysterious disease to the avian influenza outbreak in Asia and there is no\nevidence that any of the patients in question were infected with avian\ninfluenza virus. So far, the government received reports of 66 cases\naffected by the mysterious disease.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[As indicated previously Nipah and Hendra viruses are related but distinct\nparamyxoviruses which now comprise a new genus (_Henipavirus_) in the\nsub-family _Paramyxovirinae_ of the family _Paramyxoviridae_. These viruses\nhave molecular characteristics that distinguish them from previously known\nparamyxoviruses. It is likely that the virus associated with the outbreaks\nof encephalitis in Western Bangladesh is a novel member of the genus\n_Henipavirus_, in view of its epidemiological characteristics that\ndistinguish it from the two established virus species in the genus. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n The government found no link of the\nmysterious disease to the avian influenza outbreak in Asia and there is no\nevidence that any of the patients in question were infected with avian\ninfluenza virus. Four experts from WHO Geneva and Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC) of USA arrived here on Wed 4 Feb 2004 on a mission to\nprobe the source of the mysterious disease that so far has claimed 20\npeople in Bangladesh. It is likely that the virus associated with the outbreaks\nof encephalitis in Western Bangladesh is a novel member of the genus\n_Henipavirus_, in view of its epidemiological characteristics that\ndistinguish it from the two established virus species in the genus. India (North Bengal): 2001", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2203135", "date": "2003-01-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH-LIKE VIRUS - INDIA (NORTH BENGAL): 2001\n*****************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 4 Jan 2003\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: BMJ 2003;326:12 ( 4 January ) [edited]\n<http://bmj.com/cgi/content/full/326/7379/12/d?etoc>\n\n[This is further information related to the 2001 ProMED post: Undiagnosed\nencephalitis - India (North): RFI 20010827.2033]\nDirector general of the Indian Council of Medical Research Nirmal Kumar\nGanguli's announcement that the Siliguri outbreak was caused by a measles\nvariant was contradicted by researchers at the Delhi-based National\nInstitute of Communicable Diseases.\n'6 Siliguri samples were sent to [the US] Centers of Disease Control, and\nthey turned out to be of Nipah virus,' Biraj Mohan Das, director of\nemergency medical services in India's health ministry, told the BMJ. But\nresearchers at the national institute feel that the issue is still\nunresolved. 'It very well could be a new virus, although we tend to think\nit is close to Nipah virus,' a senior scientist told the BMJ.\nBut the institute's work on the Siliguri virus is at a standstill, as\nneither the institute nor any other Indian facility has the right level of\nbiosafety needed to handle dangerous pathogens. 'Such a facility is surely\nneeded in a country of India's size, and the World Health Organization\nwould extend any technical assistance India would need in this regard,'\nsaid Tej Walia, WHO representative to India.\n[Byline: Sanjay Kumar]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[We believe that this is the first official announcement that a Nipah-like\nvirus was identified in India. The Bangladesh Government confirmed that a\nNipah-like virus was present in a nearby border region (Chandpur, Sadar\nthana, Meherpur District) [Nipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001)\n20020830.5187]. At the time of the outbreak in Bangladesh, it was felt to\nbe linked to the outbreak in Siliguri, but until now there has not been\nconfirmation of identification of a Nipah-like virus from the outbreak in\nSiliguri India. More information on the laboratory findings from the\nSiliguri outbreak would be appreciated. Probably the Indian subcontinent\nviruses will differ to some extent from the Malaysian (Nipah) and\nAustralian (Hendra) viruses. These 2 viruses are unrelated to previously\ncharacterized paramyxoviruses; they are similar but not identical and are\nnow classified as species within a new genus designated _Henipavirus_. -\nMod.CP/JW/MPP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 4 Jan 2003\n At the time of the outbreak in Bangladesh, it was felt to\nbe linked to the outbreak in Siliguri, but until now there has not been\nconfirmation of identification of a Nipah-like virus from the outbreak in\nSiliguri India. \n'6 Siliguri samples were sent to [the US] Centers of Disease Control, and\nthey turned out to be of Nipah virus,' Biraj Mohan Das, director of\nemergency medical services in India's health ministry, told the BMJ. ' Probably the Indian subcontinent\nviruses will differ to some extent from the Malaysian (Nipah) and\n [We believe that this is the first official announcement that a Nipah-like\nvirus was identified in India. NIPAH-LIKE VIRUS - INDIA (NORTH BENGAL): 2001\n [Nipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001)\n it is close to Nipah virus,' a senior scientist told the BMJ.\n It very well could be a new virus, although we tend to think\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "measles", "nipah virus"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2001-03-25 00:00:00", "2003-01-04 00:00:00", "2021-01-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Meherpur District", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Delhi", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Chandpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "India", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2205630", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Nipah virus - Bangladesh (2004): WHO WER", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2205630", "date": "2004-04-23 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2004): WHO WER\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 23 Apr 2004\nFrom: Alfonso Rodriguez <arodriguezm@SaludFMV.org>\nSource: World Health Organization (WHO), Weekly Epidemiological Record, No.\n17, 2004, 79, 161-172, Fri 23 Apr 2004 [abbreviated and edited]\n<http://www.who.int/wer/2004/wer7917/en/>\n\nBangladesh: Nipah Virus Outbreaks from Jan to Apr 2004\n----------------------------------------------\nOn 17 Jan 2004, a 9-year-old child from Kazipara village was brought to the\nemergency room of Goalanda health complex in Rajbari district, Bangladesh.\nThe patient had a 3-day history of fever and worsening neurological signs.\nHe died within 15 minutes of admission. Between 17 and 21 Jan 2004, 6\nadditional children (aged between 2 and 15 years) and a 28-year-old woman\n(mother of the youngest child) also died with the same symptoms. All\npatients came from the neighboring Kazipara and Juran Molla Para villages\nand lived in 2 groups of houses located within 1 km of each other. On 21\nJan 2004, Goalanda health authorities triggered the alert and sent a report\nto the central level. On 22 Jan 2004, a joint investigation mission with\nthe Institute of Epidemiology Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) of the\nMinistry of Health, the Centre for Health and Population Research (ICDDRB),\nand the WHO country office was dispatched to Goalanda to verify the rumors\nand to collect samples for laboratory diagnosis.\nBetween 22 and 27 Jan 2004, the investigation team was able to confirm the\noutbreak and to provide a 1st description of the epidemic. Blood samples\nobtained from several patients were sent to the Special Pathogen Branch\n(SPB) at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta,\nUSA. On 4 Feb 2004, SPB-CDC detected IgM antibodies against Nipah virus in\nthe serum of 5 patients. The laboratory investigations included ELISA tests\nfor IgG and IgM antibodies, reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction\nto detect the viral genome, and viral isolation. CDC was able to culture\nthe virus from 2 of the patients. Viral RNA was amplified to show that a\nNipah virus -- with about a 95 percent homology with that isolated during\nthe Malaysian outbreak in 1999 -- was the cause of the outbreak.\nSurveillance\n------------\nIn light of the findings of previous epidemic investigations and of the\nclinical presentation of the first 8 confirmed Bangladeshi cases, 3\ncategories were employed for case definition: (1) suspect, (2) probable,\nand (3) laboratory-confirmed. In addition, when samples were collected from\nprobable and suspect cases, they were reclassified as 'non-cases' if they\nwere negative for Nipah virus IgM antibodies. Routine epidemiological\nsurveillance was reinforced by an active suspect-encephalitis\ncase-notification system -- implemented in Faridpur, Manikgonj, and Rajbari\ndistricts -- surrounding the outbreak epicenter. Surveillance was also\nreinforced by IEDCR across the entire territory.\nEpidemiology\n------------\nDescriptive epidemiology was undertaken based on the case definition. A\ncase of Nipah virus infection was any probable or laboratory-confirmed\ncase. As at 23 Feb 2004, 23 Nipah cases -- including 11\nlaboratory-confirmed cases -- had been identified in 6 districts (Faridpur,\nGolpagonj, Manikgonj, Joypurat, Naogaon, Rajbari). 17 of the 23 cases died\n(case-fatality rate of 74 percent). In all the affected districts, cases\nwere clustered geographically and in time. The 1st identified case\ndeveloped the 1st symptoms of the disease on 11 Jan 2004. Most of the\ndetected cases occurred between 12 and 25 Jan 2004 (for 20 out of 23 cases)\nsuggesting a common source of contamination. [Figure 1 (not shown here)\nillustrates the weekly distribution of cases (survivors and deaths) of\nNipah virus infection in 6 districts of Bangladesh as of 23 Feb 2004. -\nMod.CP.]\nThe main focus, with 12 Nipah cases, was identified in Goalanda, Rajbari\ndistrict, where an in-depth investigation was undertaken. Of the 12 cases,\n9 were male (75 percent) and aged under 19 years. The investigation failed\nto establish any link between these cases and sick pigs -- or other types\nof sick mammals -- as was observed during the Malaysian Nipah outbreak.\nConversely, it was noted in Goalanda that young boys collected and ate\nfruits in the trees before dawn. This observation gave rise to the\nhypothesis that contamination could have occurred while eating the same\nfruits that fruit bats (_Pteropodidae_) fed on during the night.\nSeveral epidemiological studies were conducted by the investigation team in\nGoalanda. These included a case-control study to identify risk factors for\ninfection, a seroprevalence study to assess the spread of the infection\n(antibody detection) in the general population, and a follow-up of\nconvalescent cases to assess antibody kinetics and duration of viral\nshedding. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). Laboratory investigations are\ncurrently under way at SPB-CDC, although preliminary results confirm that\nseveral _Pteropus_ flying foxes have evidence of Nipah virus infection.\nSocial mobilization and health education\n------------------\nThe Ministry of Health and the WHO team relayed all relevant information to\nthe press on a regular basis thereby keeping the public regularly informed\nabout the evolution of the situation and of investigations. Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease. The support of medical anthropologists and\nexperts in social mobilization will be needed to prepare these messages.\nCluster of Nipah Cases in Faridpur District, Mar - Apr 2004\n-----------------\nOn 19 Apr 2004, the Bangladesh Ministry of Health reported one cluster of\n30 Nipah cases, including 18 deaths from 13 Mar to 14 Apr in Faridpur\ndistrict. Laboratory testing, performed by CDC, confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection in 16 of the cases.\nThe outbreak was investigated by a team comprising experts from the IEDCR,\nICDDRB and the WHO country office. All cases lived in Guha Laksmipur\nvillage or were in contact with a spiritual leader living in this village\nwho was himself a case. Direct contact with ill patients is suspected to\nhave played a role in the transmission of the disease and the spread of\nthis outbreak.\nThe team organized infection-control and safe clinical-management workshops\nfor local medical staff in different health care settings of the Faridpur\ndistrict. Investigations are continuing.\nConclusion\n----------\nAfter Meherpur (2001) and Naogaon (2003), Bangladesh faces again an\nepidemic of Nipah. As in 2001 and 2003, few cases were identified, but this\ntime suspect cases are more widely spread throughout the country. Until\nstudies have been completed, and control and prevention measures discussed,\nthe investigation team has proposed the following short-term recommendations:\n1. Creation of a task force for Nipah outbreaks using a multidisciplinary\napproach.\n2. Creation of special funds for outbreak response and to support families\nand hospitals.\n3. Institution of urgent measures to prevent nosocomial infection in all\nhospitals of the country (patient isolation, injection safety, safe waste\ndisposal, etc.).\n4. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>", "summary": "Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease.
