"https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2198284", "date": "2000-07-22 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - MALAYSIA (SARAWAK) (03)\n*************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>", "reports": [{"diseases": ["nipah virus"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "2205260", "headline": "PRO> Nipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2205260", "date": "2004-02-02 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH/HENDRA-LIKE VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2001/2003)\n************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Mon 2 Feb 2004\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: The International Centre for Diarrhoeal Disease Research,\nHealth and Science Bulletin (English), 1(5) 2003 [edited]\n<http://www.icddrb.org/pub/publication.jsp?classificationID=0&pubID=4673>\n\nNipah/Hendra-like Virus Outbreaks, Western Bangladesh, 2001 and 2003\n--------------------------------------------------------------------\nSummary\n-------\n2 outbreaks of encephalitis, both caused by Nipah/Hendra-like\nviruses, occurred in separate areas in western Bangladesh in 2001 and\n2003. Both outbreaks occurred over brief periods and had high\ncase-fatality ratios. In contrast to previous experience with Hendra\nand Nipah viruses in which no human-to-human transmission had\noccurred in Australia, Malaysia, and Singapore, epidemiologic\ncharacteristics of the outbreak in Bangladesh suggested the\npossibility of person-to-person transmission. Exposure to animals may\nhave contributed to disease transmission to humans during the\noutbreaks, but nevertheless, the ultimate source of the virus is its\nzoonotic reservoir.\nEpidemiological Investigation\n-----------------------------\nBetween 26 Apr and 26 May 2001, 9 deaths occurred among people with\nfebrile illnesses and neurologic symptoms in Chandpur village of\nMeherpur district, 17 km from the border with India; 7 deaths\noccurred among people within the same family who lived within the\nsame home or whose homes were adjacent to each other. The mean age\nwas 40 years (range 32-60 years); 6 were males. During the same time\ninterval, 18 other residents of the village were reported to have a\nsimilar illness and survived. An investigation led by the Director\nGeneral of Health Services (DGHS) in May 2001 was followed by a 2nd\ninvestigation from 26 May to 1 Jun 2001 by a team that included staff\nfrom DGHS, the World Health Organization (WHO), and ICDDR,B. Results\nof antibody testing by enzyme-linked immunoassay (ELISA) done at the\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in the United States\nsuggested that at least 2 people in the Chandpur outbreak were\ninfected with Nipah/Hendra-like viruses.\nBetween 11 and 28 Jan 2003, another outbreak of severe illness\nincluding features of encephalitis was reported affecting at least 17\nresidents (range 4 to 42 years) of Chalksita and Biljoania villages\n(located 45 km northeast of Rajshahi) in Naogaon district; 8 people\ndied. In February 2003, an investigative team from ICDDR,B and\nCDC-Atlanta visited Chandpur to learn more about the scope and risk\nfactors for the 1st outbreak. Upon concluding studies in Chandpur,\nthe team visited Naogaon District with the objective of defining the\netiology and scope of the 2nd outbreak. The team collected specimens\nof blood from 119 residents of Chandpur and 89 residents of Chalksita\nand Biljoania villages who reportedly had been ill and recovered and\nthose who were contacts (family members, close acquaintances, and\nneighbours of cases) of residents who had died, during either of the\noutbreaks. Sera were not available from any of the patients who died\nduring the outbreaks. 4 people from Chandpur had evidence of\nantibodies (by ELISA) to Nipah antigen; all 4 had been ill during the\noutbreak period and were relatives of patients who died. Similarly,\nin Chalksita and Biljoania, 4 residents were found to have antibodies\nreactive with Nipah virus antigen; all 4 had been ill and had contact\nwith patients who died. No health care workers were identified with\nillness during the outbreak periods.\nA case of Nipah/Hendra-like virus infection was defined as a resident\nof one of the villages where the outbreaks occurred who died during\nthe outbreak periods or who had measurable antibodies to Nipah virus\nantigen. A total of 13 cases (including 9 deaths) were identified\nfrom the 1st outbreak and 12 cases (including 8 deaths) were\nidentified from the 2nd outbreak.\nFever, headache, and altered consciousness were the most common\nsymptoms. Cases in the Naogaon outbreak were more likely than\nnon-cases to have had contact with a herd of pigs that had passed\nthrough the villages. In the Meherpur outbreak, cases were more\nlikely than non-cases to report contact with a cow during the 2 weeks\nbefore illness. Close contact with ill people in the Meherpur\noutbreak, including contact with patient secretions, was reported\nmuch more commonly in cases than in non-cases. Sera were collected\nfrom a variety of animals with the goal of identifying the source of\nthe virus for these outbreaks. Antibodies reactive with Nipah virus\nantigens were found in 2 of 44 _Pteropus giganteus_ bats tested,\nwhile none were found in the remaining animals tested, including 2\nother species of bats, pigs, rodents, shrews, pigeons, and dogs in\nareas near case-households.\n(Reported by: Division of Viral and Rickettsial Disease, National\nCenter for Infectious Diseases, Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention, USA; Health Systems and Infectious Diseases Division and\nClinical Sciences Division, ICDDR,B.)\nComment and Conclusions\n------------------------\nFindings of this report suggest that Nipah/Hendra-like viruses were\nthe etiological agents of both outbreaks. Because diagnoses were\ndependent on serologic tests in both outbreaks, the precise\netiological agent is not known and may be due to a pathogen that\ncross-reacts with Nipah virus antigens in antibody assays. Hendra and\nNipah virus-associated illness was first recognized during outbreaks\nin Australia in 1994 and an epidemic in Malaysia and Singapore in\n1998-1999. A total of 105 of 276 patients with laboratory-confirmed\nNipah virus in Malaysia and Singapore died with viral encephalitis.\nClose contact with sick pigs was highly associated with human\nillness, but a few patients had no direct contact with pigs. No\nperson-to-person transmission was shown. The reservoir for Hendra and\nNipah viruses are bats of the genus _Pteropus_. In Australia, horses\nhave been the domestic link to human infections, while in Malaysia\nand Singapore, pigs were clearly the linking animal host. Antibodies\nto Nipah viruses were identified in other animals (dogs, cats, and\nhorses), but transmission from these animals to humans was not\ndocumented. Subsequent studies in Cambodia have also shown that\nevidence of infection (antibodies) with Nipah/Hendra-like viruses can\nbe found in apparently healthy bats. No cases of human illness due to\nNipah viruses were identified following the mass culling of >1\nmillion pigs in Malaysia.\nIn contrast to the Australia and the Malaysia/Singapore outbreaks, no\nclear source of transmission was identified for either of the\noutbreaks in Bangladesh summarized within this report. Because of the\nclustering of cases among family members and neighbours, the\nsecondary peaks of cases following a period of no illnesses, and an\nepidemiologic suggestion of increased risk for illness among people\nwho had exposure to secretions of other sick patients,\nperson-to-person transmission cannot be ruled out. However, absence\nof illness among health care workers creates some uncertainty about\nthis possibility. Increased risk of illness following exposure to\npigs in one outbreak and a sick cow in the other raise the potential\nof animal-to-human transmission, but this possibility cannot be\nvalidated with the limited data available.\nSystematic surveillance for encephalitis is not routinely done in\nBangladesh. It is possible that other outbreaks and sporadic cases of\nNipah/Hendra-like virus encephalitis have occurred or will occur.\nMore information will be needed to define the magnitude of the\nproblem and to identify strategies to prevent illness and deaths.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The above paper describes the outcome of investigation of 2 previous\noutbreaks of Nipah/Hendra-like virus-associated encephalitis in\nWestern Bangladesh. The paper is accompanied by figures, tables, and\nreferences, which can be accessed using the above URL. Nipah and\nHendra viruses are related but distinct paramyxoviruses which now\ncomprise a new genus (_Henipavirus_ ) in the sub-family\n_Paramyxovirinae_ of the family _Paramyxoviridae_. These viruses have\nmolecular characteristics that distinguish them from previously known\nparamyxoviruses. It is likely that the virus associated with the\noutbreaks of encephalitis in Western Bangladesh is a novel member of\nthe genus _Henipavirus_, in view of its distinctive epidemiological\ncharacteristics. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "An investigation led by the Director\nGeneral of Health Services (DGHS) in May 2001 was followed by a 2nd\ninvestigation from 26 May to 1 Jun 2001 by a team that included staff\nfrom DGHS, the World Health Organization (WHO), and ICDDR,B. MALAYSIA (SELANGOR)\n*************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\n[see also\nNipah virus - Malaysia (22) 19991024002559]\nDate: Thu, 03 Feb 2000 15:42:40 -0500\nFrom: Malaysiakini.com\nSource: <http://www.malaysiakini.com/Feb1-6/feb1/news4.htm> [edited]\n\nLast Thursday [27 Jan 2000], a pig farmer of Selangor's Batu Satu died of\nNipah [virus infection]. The fatality once again highlighted the plight of\nthe pig farmers and their families. For about 200 people who had tested\npositive for the Nipah virus, news of the death was a bad omen. There is\nmuch confusion surrounding this disclosure, and the 200 are still unclear\nabout the implications of testing positive.\n'We are not sure whether we are carriers of the actual virus or simply its\nantibodies,' said Lay Yong Tee, Chairperson of the Negeri Sembilan Nipah\nVirus Committee (NSNVC). A medical lecturer from a university told\nmalaysiakini that 'there is no evidence yet to conclude or seclude\n[exclude] whether they are carriers, but a positive reaction to the Nipah\nvirus does not mean they are sick, it simply means that they have been\ninfected.'\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n{'Testing positive' undoubtedly indicates having antibody. This is good,\nnot bad. Whether people can have antibody and be persistently infected is\nanother, unlikely, story. What is significant about this news story is that\nthe recent human infection indicates persistence of the virus in Malaysia,\npossibly in persistently infected animals, such as pigs. I wonder whether\npig retroviruses could bring about an acquired immunodeficiency state, such\nthat pigs could be persistently infected with\nNipah and other viruses. In any case, the virus has not been eradicated\nfrom Malaysia and red flags should be going up all over the place. - Mod. Pollack <pollackmp@mindspring.com> &\nChan Yow Cheong <chanyowcheong@pacific.net.sg>,\nProMED-mail Regional Moderator for Asia\nSource: media, including Malaysia Star Online\n\nHealth officials say more pigs across Malaysia will be infected by Nipah\nvirus, which has already killed more than 100 people, news reports said\ntoday.\nMalaysia has been conducting random tests on pigs throughout the Southeast\nAsian nation, according to Veterinary Services Director General Dr. Mohd\nNordin Mohd. Nor. Any hog farm found with just one infected pig [defined as\nhaving antibody, as far as we can tell - Mod.CC] must destroy its entire\nstock, he was reported as having said. Whereas Nipah virus first was\nobserved to have killed pigs and humans in Negri Sembilan state in western\nMalaysia where 84 people died, the virus has since swept into the states of\nPerak, Johor, Kelantan, Malacca and Selangor.\nHealth officials initially believed the disease was Japanese encephalitis,\n[the etiologic agent of] which is transmitted from pigs to humans by\n_Culex_ mosquitoes. Later, scientists identified a new virus, named Nipah\nafter the Malaysian village where it claimed its first victim.\nThe eight-month outbreak has ruined the pig-farming industry in many parts\nof Malaysia. Pig farmers in the worst-hit area, 60 miles southeast of the\ncapital of Kuala Lumpur, abandoned their homes in March.\nA farm in Sungai Buloh, Selangor, has been listed as 'high risk' after one\nof its workers was admitted to hospital on May 14 and fell into a coma,\nSelangor executive councillor Datuk Abdul Fatah Iskandar said yesterday.\nFatah said the Sungai Buloh farm had 2,600 pigs and 'some of its pigs are\nalso coughing, a symptom of Nipah infection.'\nHealth director-general Tan Sri Dr Abu Bakar Suleiman said the ministry\nexpected some farms outside the outbreak areas to be tested positive\nbecause 'of the way pigs were moved all over the place. 'I am more\nconcerned about farmers and workers taking precautions to protect\nthemselves from being infected by the pigs,' Dr Abu Bakar said in an\ninterview.\n[Bernama reported that] the government is no longer allowing military\npersonnel to be involved in pig culling operations and following the\ndecision, the operation to put down the animals in five pig farms in Alor\nGajah [in Malaka] today had been shelved. State Secretary Datuk Wira Abdul\nAziz Omar said confirmation of the government decision had been conveyed\nearly this morning. 'Thus the operation scheduled to commence at about 8 am\ntoday had to be shelved as there are no shooters,' he said. Pig culling\noperations planned for Perak and Johor were also facing the same problem\nfollowing the decision prohibiting members of the armed forces to be\ninvolved, he said.\nThe operation in Alor Gajah was said to be deemed urgent as the state\ngovernment feared the possibility of an increase in the number of pigs that\nhad to be destroyed as the results of the tests on blood samples taken from\npigs in 93 other farms throughout the state had yet to be received.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n[The reports painted a rather grim picture of the present status of the\nNipah virus encephalitis outbreak in peninsular Malaysia. There should be\nno doubt that Nipah virus has already spread countrywide, at least among\npigs. At least one new yet-to-be-confirmed human case (patient is now in a\ncoma) has been reported. To make matter worse, military personnel have been\ntaken off pig culling operations, resulting in delay in culling of infected\npigs because of lack of shooters. - Mod. CYC]\n[Given that wild birds are a natural amplifying host for Japanese\nencephalitis virus, what will happen next mosquito season when mosquitoes,\ninfected by feeding on such birds, look for a mammalian vertebrate blood\nmeal? If there won't be any pigs around for them to feed on will they\nquest for other large vertebrates, such as humans? - Mod.CC]\n.............................................cc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed/html>\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "nipah virus"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2021-05-14 00:00:00", "2021-03-25 00:00:00", "1999-05-19 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Malaysia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Selangor", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kuala Lumpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Malaka", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kelantan", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Johor", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Perak", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2195698", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus, remote infections - Malaysia", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2195698", "date": "1999-05-19 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS, REMOTE INFECTIONS - MALAYSIA\n*****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed/html>", "summary": "", "reports": [{"diseases": ["nipah virus"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "2195815", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus - Malaysia: official report", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2195815", "date": "1999-06-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - MALAYSIA: OFFICIAL REPORT\n***************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed/html>\n[see also\nNipah virus - Malaysia (12) 990504142537\nNipah virus - Malaysia (13) 990508222545\nNipah virus - Malaysia (14) 990513151319\nNipah virus - Malaysia (14): Disclaimer 990519122827\nNipah virus - Malaysia (15) 990518194240\nNipah virus - Malaysia (16) (not yet archived)]\nDate: Sat, 05 Jun 1999 20:40:43 -0600\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@usa.healthnet.org>\nSource: <http://www.oie.int/info/AIS_71.HTM>\n\n[Dr. Arnon Shimshony was kind enough to bring this official report to our\nattention. - Mod.CHC]\n'Emergency report' by the Director General of the Malaysian Veterinary\nServices, Dr Mohd Nordin Mohd Nor, to the OIE, published in their weekly\nDISEASE INFORMATION of 28 May 1999, Vol. 12 - No. 20.\nText of a report received on 17 May 1999 from Dr Mohd Nordin Mohd Nor,\nDirector General of Veterinary Services, Ministry of Agriculture, Kuala\nLumpur; Report date: 15 May 1999.\n1. History of human cases\nThe outbreak of Nipah disease in Malaysia, which has caused human\nfatalities, has been attributed to a viral infection in pigs. The outbreak\nin 1998/99 has been investigated and traced back to earlier incidences\nwhich were provisionally diagnosed as Japanese encephalitis (JE).\nIn 1997, the illness was reported in a number of pig-farm workers, one of\nwhom died. Most of them received treatment at private hospitals.\nIn 1998, more cases of viral encephalitis were reported and two more\nvillages were affected. A special Task Force comprising officers from the\nmedical and veterinary departments was formed. By the end of 1998, ten\nworkers from Tambun, Ulu Piah and Ampang had died from the disease after\nbeing in a coma for periods ranging from four days to a few weeks. Among\nthe viral encephalitis cases, only about 15% were confirmed as JE, leaving\nthe remaining 85% undetermined.\nBy mid-December 1998, the disease had spread to Sikamat, about 60 km south\nof Kuala Lumpur, through movement of infected pigs. Seven of the 20 workers\ndeveloped the disease and five died in January 1999.\nBy March 1999 the disease had spread to the major pig producing area of\nBukit Pelandok in the State of Negeri Sembilan.\n\nThe Department of Medical Microbiology, University of Malaya, successfully\nisolated an unknown virus. The virus was sent to the Arbovirus Research\nCenter, Center for Disease Control (CDC) in Fort Collins, Colorado, United\nStates of America. There, the Malaysian and United States scientists worked\ntogether to study the characteristics of the virus. On 18 March 1999, the\nCDC announced that the isolate was of a genus closely related to the Hendra\nvirus, a paramyxovirus first isolated in Brisbane, Australia, in 1994. On\n10 April 1999, the isolate was officially called the Nipah virus, named\nafter the village Sungai Nipah in Negeri Sembilan, where the worker from\nwhom the virus was isolated had died.\nThe disease spread to more farms and, from 1 March to 10 May 1999, a total\nof 224 suspected cases of viral encephalitis occurred in Negeri Sembilan\nwith 80 fatalities.\nOut of a total of 258 persons suspected of being infected with the Nipah\nvirus, 100 have died.\nAs a measure to control the spread of the new virus, farmers and their\nfamilies have been instructed to leave their villages and settle\ntemporarily in schools and community halls outside the affected district.\nAssistance has been sought from experts in the CDC and Australia to help\nwith the diagnosis and control of Nipah disease.\n2. Clinical picture in humans\n- Mild to severe clinical signs.\n- Fever and headaches of varying severity.\n- A few patients present drowsiness and disorientation, later falling into\na coma and requiring artificial respiration.\n- A majority of the patients in a coma subsequently die\n- The full course of the disease is still unknown.\n- The incubation period is postulated to be from one to three weeks.\nSome patients have shown serological reactivity without clinical signs.\n3. Disease in pigs\n- Generally, mortality is low but morbidity is high.\n- Spread of the disease between and within pig farms has not been established.\n3.1 Clinical signs\na) Weaners and porkers\n- Mild to severe coughing, with varying reports of mortality and morbidity.\nb) Sows and boars\n- Disease more pronounced, including moderate to severe respiratory\ndisorder characterised by dyspnoea, convulsions and death.\n- In boars, disease may be acute, death occurring within several hours.\nThick mucopurulent discharge and pneumonia in less severe forms.\nc) Piglets, gilts and sows\n- Convulsions and other neurological signs.\n3.2 Necropsy\n- Varying degrees of consolidation of the lungs, primarily the\ndiaphragmatic lobes (prominently thickened interlobular septa).\n- Cut surface showed exudate of varying consistency in the bronchi.\n- Kidneys showed congestion both on the surface and in the cortex.\n\n- Brain appeared normal except in a case with petechial haemorrhage.\n- Other visceral organs appeared normal.\n4. Disease in other animals\nSerologically positive dogs, cats, horses and goats were found in the\ninfected areas.\nDogs: lesions observed at necropsy of dogs presenting clinical signs were\nsimilar to those of affected pigs. Kidneys showed severe haemorrhage and\ncongestion. Exudates were present in the trachea and bronchi.\n5. Mode of transmission\nTransmission studies in animals are being carried out at the Australian\nAnimal Health Laboratory (AAHL), CSIRO (1), Australia.\n5.1 Oral inoculation\n- Incubation period for clinical signs was 14 to 16 days.\n- Clinical signs and gross pathology were mild.\n- Virus isolation work is in progress.\n5.2 Parenteral inoculation\n- Two inoculated pigs developed a more severe disease, one showing a\ncentral nervous system syndrome and the other a respiratory tract syndrome.\nSigns developed within 7 to 10 days of challenge.\n5.3 In-contact pigs\n- Infection occurred quickly, possibly at first contact. Neutralising\nantibodies were detected at day 14.\n- Virus multiplication in the tonsils and respiratory epithelium, together\nwith contaminated cellular debris in the lumen of air passages in the lung,\nsuggests that the virus may at least be transmitted by pharyngeal and\nbronchial secretions.\nOther results are pending.\n6. Diagnosis\n6.1 Virus isolation\nTissue samples of lung, liver, kidney, spleen, heart and brain were\ncollected from necropsied animals for virological examination. Samples were\nsent to the CDC in Atlanta, Georgia, United States of America.\nMolecular studies done on the virus isolate showed a 21% difference in the\nnucleotide sequence and an 11% difference in the amino acid sequence\ncompared to the Hendra virus.\n6.2 Serological tests\nTwo laboratories, namely the Veterinary Research Institute (VRI), Ipoh, and\nthe Task Force Laboratory at the Department of Medical Microbiology,\nUniversity of Malaya, were selected to carry out serological tests on the\nveterinary and human sera respectively. Samples were tested using the IgG\nand IgM capture ELISA at the above laboratories and virus neutralisation\ntests at AAHL, Geelong (Australia).\n\n7. Results of serological surveillance in animals during the outbreak period\n7.1 Pigs\nIn a previously infected farm, more than 95% of sows had Nipah virus\nantibodies. More than 90% of the piglets had antibodies assumed to be\nmaternal antibodies. Antibody prevalence across all ages is currently being\nstudied in an infected farm.\n7.2 Horses\nAt a farm in an infected area, 2 of the 47 polo horses were found to have\nNipah virus antibodies and were euthanised. All racehorses in the country\nhave tested negative.\n7.3 Dogs\nMore than 50% of dogs captured in one of the infected areas were positive\nto Nipah virus by IgG capture ELISA using Hendra antigen.\nAntibodies in dogs in other infected areas are also being studied.\n7.4 Cats\nOut of 23 cats tested in the affected area, one was seropositive.\n7.5 Bats\nOut of 99 flying foxes tested, 15 were found to be positive to the virus\nneutralisation test for the Nipah virus.\n7.6 Rodents\nTo date, serum samples from rats trapped in infected areas have all been\nnegative. Further tests are in progress.\n7.7 Other animals\nSera have also been collected from cattle, goats, sheep, squirrels, wild\nboar, wild birds, poultry and ostriches for later testing.\n8. Control and eradication programme\n8.1 Control Programme for Nipah Disease Phase I\nWith the discovery of the aetiology of the disease, a programme was\ndeveloped for immediate eradication of the disease by mass culling of pigs.\n>From 28 February to 26 April 1999, a total of 901,918 pigs were culled in\nfour infected areas (one in the State of Perak, one in Selangor and two in\nNegeri Sembilan).\n8.2 Control Programme for Nipah Disease Phase II\nA surveillance programme was developed to detect previously infected farms\nby testing for the presence of pigs with antibodies to the Nipah virus.\nAll farms outside the previously designated high risk areas will be\nscreened by taking a statistically valid random sample of sows for\ndetection of Nipah virus antibodies. Farms with two consecutive negative\ntests performed within an interval of three weeks will be accorded\n'provisionally free' status. However, farms found to be positive during the\nfirst or second tests will be depopulated.\nTo date, 235 farms have been tested, of which 9 were found to be positive.\nA total of 23,736 animals and 11,458 pigs in 4 of the positive farms have\nbeen culled. In this programme a total of 824 farms with a 1.6 million SPP\n(standing pig population) will be tested.\n9. Actions for the future\nInvestigations will continue in order to ascertain the natural hosts of the\nNipah virus and the modes of transmission to domestic animals and humans.\nThe outbreak has given the Government of Malaysia an opportunity to review\nthe status of the pig industry and the direction for revival of the industry.\n(1) CSIRO: Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n[This is the first comprehensive report we have seen and may be the first\ncomprehensive report. Japanese encephalitis is no longer being reported as\nthe etiologic agent of Nipah disease and other steps forward obviously have\nbeen taken. The overall mortality rate in humans is 38.8%. Many questions\nremain (Are pigs persistently infected? Does Nipah virus react with\nantibodies to paramyxoviruses other than Hendra virus? What is the\nsignificance of antibody if 'some patients have shown serological\nreactivity without clinical signs'? and more). Nonetheless, the picture is\nclearing. - Mod.CHC]\n..........................chc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. - Malaysia", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2195696", "date": "1999-05-19 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS, FRUIT BAT RESERVOIR? - MALAYSIA\n********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed/html>", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed/html>", "reports": [{"diseases": ["nipah virus"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "2195686", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus, Australian TV report", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2195686", "date": "1999-05-19 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS, AUSTRALIAN TV REPORT\n*********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed/html>\nDate: Tue, 18 May 1999 22:08:46 +1000\nFrom: Niall Byrne <niall@byc.com.au>\n\nABC TV (Australian Broadcasting Corporation) presented a half-hour report\non Nipah virus followed by an interview with Stephen Morse. The report,\nfilmed in Malaysia, included much footage from infected properties\nincluding the first infected property. The program also claimed, I think on\nthe basis of retrospective serology, that the first human cases were\nactually in November 1997. Pollack <pollackmp@mindspring.com> &\nChan Yow Cheong <chanyowcheong@pacific.net.sg>,\nProMED-mail Regional Moderator for Asia\nSource: media, including Malaysia Star Online\n\nHealth officials say more pigs across Malaysia will be infected by Nipah\nvirus, which has already killed more than 100 people, news reports said\ntoday.\nMalaysia has been conducting random tests on pigs throughout the Southeast\nAsian nation, according to Veterinary Services Director General Dr. Mohd\nNordin Mohd. Nor. Any hog farm found with just one infected pig [defined as\nhaving antibody, as far as we can tell - Mod.CC] must destroy its entire\nstock, he was reported as having said. Whereas Nipah virus first was\nobserved to have killed pigs and humans in Negri Sembilan state in western\nMalaysia where 84 people died, the virus has since swept into the states of\nPerak, Johor, Kelantan, Malacca and Selangor.\nHealth officials initially believed the disease was Japanese encephalitis,\n[the etiologic agent of] which is transmitted from pigs to humans by\n_Culex_ mosquitoes. Later, scientists identified a new virus, named Nipah\nafter the Malaysian village where it claimed its first victim.\nThe eight-month outbreak has ruined the pig-farming industry in many parts\nof Malaysia. Pig farmers in the worst-hit area, 60 miles southeast of the\ncapital of Kuala Lumpur, abandoned their homes in March.\nA farm in Sungai Buloh, Selangor, has been listed as 'high risk' after one\nof its workers was admitted to hospital on May 14 and fell into a coma,\nSelangor executive councillor Datuk Abdul Fatah Iskandar said yesterday.\nFatah said the Sungai Buloh farm had 2,600 pigs and 'some of its pigs are\nalso coughing, a symptom of Nipah infection.'\nHealth director-general Tan Sri Dr Abu Bakar Suleiman said the ministry\nexpected some farms outside the outbreak areas to be tested positive\nbecause 'of the way pigs were moved all over the place. 'I am more\nconcerned about farmers and workers taking precautions to protect\nthemselves from being infected by the pigs,' Dr Abu Bakar said in an\ninterview.\n[Bernama reported that] the government is no longer allowing military\npersonnel to be involved in pig culling operations and following the\ndecision, the operation to put down the animals in five pig farms in Alor\nGajah [in Malaka] today had been shelved. State Secretary Datuk Wira Abdul\nAziz Omar said confirmation of the government decision had been conveyed\nearly this morning. 'Thus the operation scheduled to commence at about 8 am\ntoday had to be shelved as there are no shooters,' he said. Pig culling\noperations planned for Perak and Johor were also facing the same problem\nfollowing the decision prohibiting members of the armed forces to be\ninvolved, he said.\nThe operation in Alor Gajah was said to be deemed urgent as the state\ngovernment feared the possibility of an increase in the number of pigs that\nhad to be destroyed as the results of the tests on blood samples taken from\npigs in 93 other farms throughout the state had yet to be received.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n[The reports painted a rather grim picture of the present status of the\nNipah virus encephalitis outbreak in peninsular Malaysia. There should be\nno doubt that Nipah virus has already spread countrywide, at least among\npigs. At least one new yet-to-be-confirmed human case (patient is now in a\ncoma) has been reported. To make matter worse, military personnel have been\ntaken off pig culling operations, resulting in delay in culling of infected\npigs because of lack of shooters. - Mod. Calisher' <calisher@usa.healthnet.org>\n\nIf you are getting as tired as I am of seeing all these messages\nabout pig-killing exercises, ill-defined diagnoses, and unscientific\nstatements attributed to uninformed politicians, please see my\ncomments below.\nI refer to, for example:\n>>>>>>>>>>\nDate: Sun 9 May 1999 17:01:19 +0000\nFrom: Jamal Hassan <JAMAL@jphvri.po.my>\nSource: The New Straits Times Press (Malaysia) Berhad;\nSun, May 9 1999;\n<http://www.nstpi.com.my/nstpi/>http://www.nstpi.com.my/nstpi/\n[excerpts]\nPreparations are under way for the culling of an estimated\n4000 pigs from a farm in Seelong, near Senai, after blood\nsamples from pigs there tested positive for the Nipah virus...\nDr Chua said 'The public at large is not at risk because the\naffected farm is deep in an oil palm estate and there are no\nhuman settlements within a five-kilometre radius.  'The other\n81 farms in Johor are located in remote areas, at least 15\nkilometres from any residential area.So there is no reason\nto panic,' he stressed.  A 0.5km radius of the farm has been\nsealed off and the police will deploy a 24-hour road-block near\nthe farm...\nOn blood samples taken from the farmer and seven workers\nfrom the pig farm in Seelong, Dr Chua said the WHO collaborating\ncentre for air-borne [sic -- should read 'arthropod-borne,' meaning\ncarried by mosquitoes, ticks, etc.] viruses in Universiti Malaya had\nconfirmed them negative for the Nipah virus. Dr Chua added that a\nnearby vegetable farm had also been asked to cease operations\nimmediately.\nHealth Minister Datuk Chua Jui Meng said today only 31\nsuspected JE cases were currently being treated in various\nhospitals in the country...\n'A total of 258 people who were suspected VE cases were\nadmitted at the height of the outbreak in March of which 100\ndied while another 127 had been discharged,' he said...\nHe also reminded residents in VE affected areas in Lukut not\nto return to their houses for the moment until the Cabinet Task\nForce has made a decision on the matter...\nMeanwhile, the JE Humanitarian Fund today received RM30,000 ...\nfor relief work for 204 families affected by the viral encephalitis\noutbreak in Bukit Pelandok.\n>>>>>>>>>>\n>From the very outset of this Nipah virus epidemic -- whenever\nthat was -- we have been bombarded with misinformation and\nmisstatements.  I have not seen or heard of a shred of evidence\nthat there has been a single confirmed case of Japanese\nencephalitis [in peninsular Malaysia] since very early 1999,\neither in humans or in pigs. \nIf such data are available, we would welcome them and would\npost them immediately on ProMED-mail.\nThe basic problem remains definitions for 'a case' and 'an\ninfection with'. Antibody to a virus in a serum sample from an\nill person or pig (or horse or anything else) is confirmatory of\nnothing.  Yet the Malaysian authorities are reported to have\npublicly pronounced this many cases of JE virus and that many\ncases of Nipah virus.  A confirmed 'positive', in terms of a human\nor a pig, depends upon either virus isolation or a change in\nantibody titers between paired acute- and convalescent-phase\nserum samples. \nNo doubt finding IgM antibody in a single serum sample from a\nsick human (or pig) provides excellent presumptive data, but it\ndoes not provide confirmatory evidence of recent infection.\nWe have heard nothing regarding persistence of Nipah virus in\npigs.  If there is evidence that persistent infections occur, we\nwould like to know that -- as would everyone else.  If there is no\nevidence for such a persistent state, then why are pigs that are\n'positive' (assuming this means they have been shown to have\nantibody in a single serum sample) being killed?  Seems to me\nthese immune animals would be the ones to retain.\nUntil we begin to receive real data, rather than statements from\ngovernment spokespeople, ProMED-mail will not post repetitive\nand unhelpful reports, but will continue to post messages regarding\nareas newly discovered to have infected animals, and other\nimportant data. \nNo further mention of Japanese encephalitis will be made here\nunless some confirmatory evidence is presented that JE virus is\nstill circulating or has infected even one human or one pig since,\nsay, January 1999.\nTerms such as 'VE', meaning 'virus encephalitis', are\nobfuscating with regard to whether a human or a pig has\nJapanese encephalitis or Nipah virus.\nThe Malaysian government task force responsible for\ninvestigations of the epizoodemic of Nipah disease is still\ncalled the 'State Japanese encephalitis/Nipah Task Force',\nwhich suggests that JE is not only a current problem but\nTHE principal curent problem. It could as well be called\nthe State Ebola/Yellow Fever/Nipah Task Force and be just\nas accurate. Indeed, The New Straits Times Press (Malaysia)\nBerhad, Sat 24 Apr 1999) quoted Health Minister Chua as\nhaving said that there were more than 13 000 malaria cases\nlast year but no yellow fever cases. He could have added no\nsmallpox either.\nPersonally, I suspect that the actual situation with regard to\nthe causes of this epizoodemic, the political reasons and\nrepercussions deriving therefrom, and the overall logistics\nhave not begun to be mentioned.  I have received, addressed\nto me personally, many messages from people in Malaysia\nwho have told me of scientific personnel in Malaysia being\nthreatened with firing, transfers, and funding reductions if they\ncontinue to speak out in conflict with official statements, and of\nother even more heinous accusations. \nThe free exchange of information can never cause the\nexpansion of an epidemic, and statements that the public\nshould not panic cannot begin to assuage public feelings\nthat the entire story is not being told.\n--\nCharles H.
